‚ĚĮ docs/wip                                                                      

‚óŹ I've read both WIP docs ‚ÄĒ docs/wip/ has only migration-derived-placement.md
  (the derived-access placement/scheduling sketch) besides notes.md, which I've
  left alone. Pairing it with docs/design/the-flat-effect-model.md (your current
  branch's anchor doc), here's the discussion.

  The claim, stated sharply

  In arche, every capability is an explicit ask paid for by surrendering 
  something ‚ÄĒ and the ask is structural, never inferred:

  - Effect power. Ask = be a system/each. Price = you stop being a value (no
  params, unspellable as a call, only the schedule reaches you). The flat-effect
  doc is emphatic that this color is written, not inferred ‚ÄĒ "an inferred color
  is a hidden color" (line¬†153).
  - Branch power. Ask = escalate map ‚Üí each/system. Price = you lose free
  parallel/GPU placement. A map is "pure, branch-free"; control flow is E0046.
  To branch you must climb to a kind that also carries effect power.

  So branch power and effect power are bundled into the same escalation today:
  the moment you want a data-dependent if, you land in each/system, which is the
  effect leaf, which is CPU-bound.

  Where this bites the migration doc

  migration-derived-placement.md ¬ß0 says game.step is "branch-free, so a GPU 
  kernel candidate" ‚ÄĒ phrased as a fact derived by inspecting the body. But for
  a map that's not derived at all; it's guaranteed by the kind you already asked
  for. Every map is branch-free by definition (E0046 enforces it). The doc's
  own framing ("the compiler derives per-column facts from the body") quietly
  slides branch-freedom into the "sniffed from the body" bucket ‚ÄĒ which is
  exactly the inferred color the flat-effect doc condemns.

  The reconciliation, and I think your point: GPU-legality is granted by the 
  asked-for kind, not derived from the body. You made the explicit ask when you
  wrote map (renouncing branches + effects). That's the "explicit ask for power"
  ‚ÄĒ symmetric with system.

  This actually shrinks the ¬ß5 research worry. Split the placement decision in
  two:
  - Legality (can this go to the GPU at all?) ‚ÄĒ decided by the explicit kind 
  ask. No body analysis, no inference, no research. map ‚áí legal; each/system ‚áí
  not (effects/divergence).
  - Profitability (should it?) ‚ÄĒ the cost model. This is the genuinely-open
  static-over-non-affine-columnar part.

  The explicit-power framing pulls legality out of the unsolved zone entirely
  and leaves only the cost question open.

  The ladder is the same ladder

  The effect model already grades capability ‚ÄĒ applicative ‚Üí selective
  (whenS/ifS, conditional execution of a static set, lowers to a guarded branch)
  ‚Üí monadic (data-dependent shape, banned as a value). Branch power has the
  identical gradient, and it maps onto placement:

  ‚ĒĆ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēź

  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā monadic   ‚Ēā system/each      ‚Ēā data-dependent       ‚Ēā CPU leaf          ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā           ‚Ēā runs+branches    ‚Ēā control flow         ‚Ēā                   ‚Ēā
  ‚ĒĒ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēė

  The middle rung is the interesting one and the reason "branch permission" is
  worth naming as its own ask. Today map is all-or-nothing: zero branches
  (E0046) or escalate to a CPU effect leaf. But there's a real, GPU-legal middle
  ‚ÄĒ predicated/select branching (clamp, min/max, masked write ‚ÄĒ a conditional
  whose control flow is static even though the value varies). That's the
  data-parallel analogue of the selective rung. Making it an explicit, graded 
  ask ("I want predicated branch power, which stays GPU-legal") rather than
  folding it into the map‚Üíeach cliff is the symmetric move with the effect
  model.

  My read of the two ways to take this

  1. Conservative / corrective: branch-freedom is already an explicit ask (the
  kind), so the migration doc's "derived from the body" language is the bug. Fix
  it to "GPU-legality is granted by the asked-for kind; only profitability is
  derived," and note the symmetry with effect coloring. Smaller, purely a doc
  reframe ‚ÄĒ no language change.
  2. Generative / extending: introduce a graded branch capability for map ‚ÄĒ an
  explicit ask for predicated/select power that stays GPU-legal, distinct from
  full data-dependent control flow (which still escalates to each/system). This
  is a real language proposal mirroring the selective rung, not just a doc fix.

‚ĚĮ just do analysis for now if its even helpful                                  
  or papers on this subject                                                     

‚óŹ Skipping the doc edit ‚ÄĒ here's the analysis and the literature, which is the
  more useful thing here.

  The one genuinely novel cell

  Lay the design space on two axes and almost everything you're proposing
  already exists except one combination:

  - Who decides GPU legality ‚ÄĒ body inference vs. type/kind declaration
  - How the branch question is handled ‚ÄĒ static divergence analysis vs. graded
  explicit ask vs. runtime

  The migration doc's ¬ß0 ("branch-free, so a candidate") sits in body-inference 
  + static analysis. Your instinct ("explicit ask for power") moves it to
  type/kind-declaration + graded explicit ask. That specific cell ‚ÄĒ graded, 
  type-level branch capability over non-affine columnar/ECS data, mapped onto 
  the applicative‚Üíselective‚Üímonad ladder ‚ÄĒ is the part I can't find assembled
  anywhere. Every neighboring cell is solved. So the framing is sound; the
  contribution is the assembly, not any single piece.

  Papers, grouped by the thread they ground

  1. The ladder itself (your backbone, already cited).
  Mokhov, Lukyanov, Marlow, Dimino, Selective Applicative Functors, ICFP 2019.
  The load-bearing one: selective = conditional execution of a statically-known
  effect set, no bind. That is exactly predication ‚ÄĒ a guarded branch whose
  control flow is static even when the value varies. This is the formal reason
  "predicated/select branching is GPU-safe" and "data-dependent shape is not"
  are the same distinction as applicative-vs-monadic. If you write up the graded
  branch power, this is the citation that says the middle rung is a real, named
  algebra, not a hack.

  2. The branch question, as a static analysis (the thing you'd be replacing 
  with an ask).
  - Allen, Kennedy, Porterfield, Warren, Conversion of control dependence to 
  data dependence, POPL 1983 ‚ÄĒ origin of if-conversion (turning a branch into
  predication). The mechanism under "selective lowers to a guarded branch."
  - Coutinho, Sampaio, Pereira, Meira, Divergence Analysis and Optimizations,
  PACT 2011; Sampaio et al., Divergence Analysis, ACM TOPLAS¬†2013. This is the 
  closest prior art to what ¬ß0 proposes to derive ‚ÄĒ a static analysis that
  classifies each branch as uniform (cheap, GPU-fine) vs. divergent
  (warp-splitting). arche's move is to stop analyzing divergence and instead
  make non-divergent branching an explicit ask (the map/selective rung) and
  divergent branching an escalation (each/system). Frame your contribution
  against this paper directly: "explicit graded ask in place of divergence
  analysis."
  - Fung, Sham, Yuan, Aamodt, Dynamic Warp Formation, MICRO 2007 ‚ÄĒ why
  divergence costs what it costs (the runtime story you avoid by static gating).

  3. GPU-legality from the type, not the body (precedent for your reframe).
  - Chakravarty, Keller, Lee, McDonell, Grover, Accelerating Haskell Array Codes
  with Multicore GPUs, DAMP 2011, and McDonell et al., Optimising Purely 
  Functional GPU Programs, ICFP 2013 ‚ÄĒ Accelerate. The Acc/Exp type
  distinguishes GPU-runnable array computation from host code. This is literally
  "the type announces GPU-eligibility" ‚ÄĒ the structural twin of Eff announcing
  effect color. Your strongest analogy: legality is a color in the type, paid
  for by surrendering expressiveness (no arbitrary recursion/control flow inside
  Exp).
  - Henriksen, Serup, Elsman, Henglein, Oancea, Futhark, PLDI 2017 ‚ÄĒ a pure
  data-parallel language compiled to GPU; the restriction to a parallel-safe 
  subset is the language design, exactly arche's "map renounces branches/effects
  to earn placement."
  - CUDA's __device__/__global__/__host__ qualifiers ‚ÄĒ the crude, real-world
  version of "ask for the power by writing the qualifier." Worth a sentence as
  the existence proof that kind-granted legality is normal practice, just
  ungraded.

  4. Capabilities/effects as explicit asks (the philosophical anchor).
  - Brachth√§user, Schuster, Ostermann, Effects as Capabilities, OOPSLA 2020 ‚ÄĒ
  effects handled by capabilities passed explicitly rather than inferred. This
  is the precise PL-theory name for "power is an explicit ask, never a hidden
  color," and it's the citation that makes your map‚Üíeach escalation a capability
  discipline, not just a kind hierarchy.
  - Peyton Jones, Wadler, Imperative Functional Programming, POPL 1993 (IO
  monad) and Levy's Call-by-Push-Value ‚ÄĒ both already in the doc; CBPV is your
  value/computation = func/system split, and it's also the right lens for
  "predicated map is still a value; divergent each is a computation."

  5. The part you correctly bracket as open (profitability, not legality).
  - StarPU (Augonnet et al., Euro-Par¬†2009), Legion + mapper (Bauer et al.,
  SC¬†2012), Qilin (Luk et al., MICRO¬†2009), Polly-ACC (Grosser & Hoefler,
  ICS¬†2016). All cited or adjacent. The honest note: every one of these decides
  where via runtime or affine-only cost models. None decides it statically over
  non-affine columnar data with no runtime scheduler ‚ÄĒ which is your ¬ß5 caveat,
  and it survives the reframe untouched. The reframe only removes legality from
  the open set; profitability stays open.

  The analytical payoff, in one line

  Splitting legality (explicit, type/kind-granted, no analysis) from
  profitability (derived cost model, genuinely open) does two things: it makes
  the migration doc consistent with the flat-effect ethos ("an inferred color is
  a hidden color" ‚ÄĒ so don't sniff branch-freedom, grant it), and it shrinks
  the ¬ß5 research surface to exactly the cell no prior system occupies. The
  selective-functor ladder is what proves the middle rung (predicated/GPU-safe
  branching) is a principled stop, not an arbitrary one.

  Two honest caveats on the above: the citations are from memory (classic enough
  that I'm confident on authors/venues, but I'd verify exact titles/years
  before they go in a doc), and the Accelerate/Acc-Exp analogy is the strongest
  one ‚ÄĒ the graded branch ask (three rungs, not two) is where you're genuinely
  past the prior art.

  Want me to verify any of these against the actual papers, or pull the
  divergence-analysis / selective-functors ones in full to check how cleanly the
  "predication = selective rung" mapping really holds?

‚ĚĮ so my systems are like StarPU codelets?                                       

‚óŹ Short answer: no ‚ÄĒ the StarPU codelet's closest arche analogue is your map, 
  not your system. And the analogy holds only on the data-access axis; on the
  two axes that define the flat-effect model it actively inverts. Worth walking
  through because the mismatch is informative.

  What a codelet actually is

  A starpu_codelet bundles three things: (1) multiple implementations of one
  computation (cpu_funcs[], cuda_funcs[], opencl_funcs[]), (2) a buffer count + 
  access modes (STARPU_R / STARPU_W / STARPU_RW / STARPU_SCRATCH /
  STARPU_REDUX), and (3) a perf model. A task is an instance: codelet + specific
  data handles. You submit tasks; a runtime scheduler reads the perf model +
  data-locality, picks the device, inserts transfers, and maintains coherence
  across memory nodes via an MSI-like DSM. Dependencies are derived from access
  modes on handles (RAW/WAR/WAW), sequential-consistency by default.

  Why it's map, not system

  A codelet is, by design, pure offloadable compute that touches no I/O. Doing a
  syscall inside a codelet is an anti-pattern ‚ÄĒ it blocks the worker, breaks
  the cost model, and can't go to the GPU. That is exactly the rung your map
  occupies (branch-free, effect-free, placement-eligible) and exactly what a
  system is forbidden from being. A system is the effect leaf ‚ÄĒ it runs effects,
  branches on results, inserts/deletes. So system is the one arche kind that is
  least like a codelet; it's the thing codelets are specifically not allowed to
  be.

  This falls straight out of the capability ladder from before: only the rung 
  that renounced branches+effects (map) can be a codelet. Keep the effect power
  (system) and you're disqualified ‚ÄĒ same reason it's CPU-bound.

  The three places it inverts

  ‚ĒĆ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēź

  ‚Ēā Access    ‚Ēā declared per task            ‚Ēā derived from the kernel     ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā modes     ‚Ēā (STARPU_R/W/RW)              ‚Ēā body (the ¬ß0 pitch: no      ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā           ‚Ēā                              ‚Ēā annotations)                ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§

  ‚Ēā           ‚Ēā codelet = pure compute; I/O  ‚Ēā effects are a first-class   ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā Effects   ‚Ēā is an anti-pattern           ‚Ēā kind (system), just not the ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā           ‚Ēā                              ‚Ēā  offloadable one            ‚Ēā
  ‚ĒĒ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēė

  The access-mode row is the subtle one and ties back to our earlier thread:
  StarPU makes access modes an explicit ask (you write STARPU_R); arche wants to
  derive them. That's the same "explicit color vs. inferred color" tension you
  flagged ‚ÄĒ and it's why the legality-from-explicit-kind, profitability-derived
  split is the clean reconciliation: map-vs-system is your explicit ask (like
  the codelet's device-eligibility), while the per-column R/W facts are the
  derived part (where you diverge from StarPU, for better or worse).

  What genuinely is codelet-like in arche

  Two things, and both live in the data layer, not in system:

  1. (kernel + per-column access modes) ‚Üí derived dependency graph (¬ß0‚Äď1). Real
  correspondence ‚ÄĒ StarPU's implicit-dependency machinery does the same job,
  just from declared modes at runtime instead of derived modes at compile time.
  2. Read-only replication (¬ß3) ‚ÜĒ STARPU_R data replicated-not-copied-back. The
  migration doc already cites this directly.

  And the unit you name in #run (a map or system) is closer to a StarPU task (a
  schedulable instance) than to a codelet (the reusable kernel description) ‚ÄĒ
  arche doesn't separate those two the way StarPU does.

  So the precise statement: your map is a compile-time, derived-access, 
  no-runtime-scheduler codelet; your system is the effect leaf a codelet is 
  forbidden to be. The reason StarPU is in the migration doc's ¬ß5 at all is that
  it's the closest prior art for the placement decision ‚ÄĒ but it makes that
  decision at runtime, which is exactly the fallback arche denies itself, and
  that denial is what pushes the map-placement problem into the unsolved cell.

  The leaf world is one kind + a permission set

  - eff ‚ÄĒ run an Eff / insert / delete (the effect power)
  - branch ‚ÄĒ data-dependent control flow

  Every named kind is just a corner of that cube:

  ‚ĒĆ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēź

  So your claim, stated tightly: map is an each with the eff and branch 
  permissions revoked. (Same fan; effects and branches denied.) The keyword map
  is sugar for "the per-row leaf at the floor of the permission lattice" ‚ÄĒ and
  that floor is exactly what makes it GPU-legal. This closes the whole thread
  from before: GPU-legality isn't sniffed from the body, it's read off the 
  permission set ‚ÄĒ "you didn't ask for eff or branch." That's the explicit-ask
  answer to the migration doc's "derived from the body" inference problem.

  The one place "everything is a system minus permissions" overreaches

  func is not a permission-restricted system. The leaf kinds are scheduled, 
  never called (no params, can't be under-applied) ‚ÄĒ that's the structural fact
  that makes "system can't call system" unspellable, the keystone of the whole
  flat model. func is callable (it nests, recurses). That's the
  call-by-push-value value/computation line, not a revocable permission. You
  can't get func by removing powers from system; you'd have to add callability,
  which would collapse flatness. So: every scheduled leaf is a system minus 
  permissions; func is the value world next to the lattice, not a point in it.

  The payoff: the lattice exposes a hole

  The cube has 3 named corners out of 8. The interesting empty one is (fan ‚úď, 
  eff ‚úó, branch ‚úď) ‚ÄĒ a pure per-row kernel that branches but runs no effects.
  That's GPU-legal-with-divergence: the predicated/selective rung from earlier.
  It has no name today, so to get a branch you must escalate map ‚Üí each, which
  also hands you eff ‚ÄĒ a power you didn't want, and the very power that forces
  CPU placement. map ‚Üí each over-grants: it bundles branch and eff even though
  the effect model treats them as independent (selective vs. monadic).

  So "branch as an explicit graded ask" isn't a new feature bolted on ‚ÄĒ it's
  un-bundling a permission the lattice already shows is bundled. Naming that
  empty corner is the graded-branch-power proposal, and it falls straight out of
  your reframing.

‚ĚĮ the entire design proposal was about JUST having system with rust style       
  semantics for deciding where memory and stuff belong                          

‚óŹ Got it ‚ÄĒ I was building a kind-zoo (map/each as primitive corners) when the
  proposal collapses that: one kind, system, and a Rust-style borrow analysis 
  over how each system touches columns decides where data lives. The kinds
  aren't the design; the borrow semantics are. Let me re-center on that and say
  what's actually load-bearing.

  The mapping, stated as Rust

  A system touches columns. The access is the borrow:

  - read a column = &T (shared borrow)
  - write a column = &mut T (exclusive borrow)

  And the single Rust rule ‚ÄĒ aliasing XOR mutability ‚ÄĒ does double duty:

  1. Scheduling/parallelism. Disjoint &mut sets (or overlap only on &) ‚áí systems
  run concurrently; an &mut conflict serializes. This is literally Bevy ‚ÄĒ
  Rust's borrow rules applied to ECS system params (Query<&T> vs Query<&mut T>).
  Solved prior art; lift it.
  2. Placement / residency / transfer. The same borrow facts decide where memory
  belongs:
    - a column only ever &-borrowed across the frame ‚Üí read-only, so upload
  once, replicate, share, never copy back (Rust's &/Sync ‚ÄĒ shareable precisely
  because no one can mutate it).
    - a column &mut-borrowed every frame ‚Üí exclusively owned, hot ‚Üí keep
  resident on the device that writes it.
    - a borrow that crosses a device boundary ‚Üí the transfer point (a
  reborrow/move across memory nodes).

  So "map" isn't a kind you write ‚ÄĒ it's a derived property of a system whose
  borrow signature happens to be read-mostly + effect-free + branch-free, which
  is what makes it GPU-placeable. The keyword vanishes; the borrow facts subsume
  it. That's the correction landing: don't name the corners, read them off the
  borrows.

  What this cleanly partitions (and why it matters)

  The Rust framing draws the exact line through the migration doc's ¬ß5:

  - borrows ‚Üí schedule is solved. Rust/Bevy already use ownership+borrow for
  correctness and thread parallelism. No research.
  - borrows ‚Üí device residency + transfer, statically, is the novel reach. Rust
  decides stack/heap/drop from ownership; it has never decided CPU-vs-GPU
  residency from &/&mut. Bevy parallelizes across threads, not across a PCIe
  boundary. So using the identical borrow facts to place data on a device and
  schedule transfers at compile time ‚ÄĒ over non-affine columnar storage, no
  runtime scheduler ‚ÄĒ is the part with no precedent.

  That's the whole pitch in one sentence: Rust's borrow checker, but the thing 
  it decides is device residency and transfer, not stack/heap and drop. The
  &/&mut distinction you already need for the schedule is the same signal that
  says "replicate this read-only column to the GPU once" vs. "pin this hot
  column where its writer runs." You get the scheduling for free from prior art,
  and the only genuinely open question is whether the borrow lattice carries
  enough information to place residency statically without a runtime cost model
  ‚ÄĒ which is exactly where StarPU/Legion fall back to runtime and arche refuses
  to.

  Is that the framing you were after ‚ÄĒ Rust ownership repurposed from
  memory-lifetime to device-placement, one system kind, everything else derived?

‚úĽ Worked for 1m 18s

‚ĚĮ does the mut belong in the query?                                             
                                                                                
  also its not just for borrowing, its for permissions in general. Like         
  permission to run effects, etc                                                

‚óŹ Two things, and the second reframes the first.

  Does mut belong in the query? Yes ‚ÄĒ and separate compilation forces it.

  In Bevy you write Query<&mut Position, &Velocity> ‚ÄĒ the mutability lives in
  the query type, not derived from the body. arche should do the same, for a
  reason stronger than mimicry: the scheduler and placer must read a system's 
  signature, never its body. Bevy's scheduler parallelizes by inspecting query
  types alone; it never opens the system body. That's the whole reason it scales
  and composes.

  This is the decisive argument against the migration doc's current "derived
  from the body" pitch (¬ß0). If write-facts are derived by inspecting the body,
  then:
  - you can't decide placement without the body ‚Üí separate compilation breaks
  (and you care about separate compilation),
  - and you've re-introduced the inferred color the flat-effect doc condemns
  ("an inferred color is a hidden color").

  Declaring &mut pos in the query fixes both: it's the system's signature, the
  placer consumes the signature, and the body is checked against the declaration
  (Rust borrow-check: write an undeclared column ‚Üí error; declare mut you never
  write ‚Üí unused-mut lint). Declaration + checking, not inference.

  And critically this does not violate "no annotations" ‚ÄĒ that pitch was always
  about no @gpu, no device names, no placement directives. &mut in a query isn't
  a placement annotation; it's the access signature the placer reads to derive
  placement. So the honest split is: permissions are declared; placement is 
  derived from them. Same legality-vs-profitability line as before.

  One boundary: a query is column-scoped, so it carries only the data
  permissions. Effect permission isn't about a column ‚ÄĒ so it lives on the
  system, not the query (below).

  It's a capability set, not just borrow

  Right ‚ÄĒ generalize &/&mut into a full permission lattice on one system. The
  axes:

  - data (column-scoped ‚Üí in the query): read (&) vs write (&mut) per column
  - effects (‚Üí on the system signature): permission to run an Eff
  - structural (‚Üí system): permission to insert/delete
  - ambient (‚Üí system): permission to read singleton/global pools

  So a system declares something like system reads{pos} writes{vel} runs{io}
  (sketch) ‚ÄĒ query for the column caps, a capability clause for the rest. And
  then the punchline that closes the whole thread:

  The kinds ‚ÄĒ func, map, each, system ‚ÄĒ are named presets over this capability 
  set, not primitives.

  - map = system with { writes some columns, no effects, no structural, no 
  ambient, no branch } ‚Üí the preset that happens to be GPU-placeable.
  - each = map's presets + { effects, branch }.
  - func = the one that isn't a preset (it's callable/value-world, not a
  scheduled leaf ‚ÄĒ the CBPV line, the keystone of flatness).

  That's exactly your "JUST system": the kind keywords dissolve into points in a
  declared capability lattice, and "is this GPU-legal" becomes "does its
  capability set lack the effect and branch permissions" ‚ÄĒ read straight off the
  signature, no body analysis, separately compilable.

  So mut-in-the-query is just the first capability to move into the signature;
  effects and structural mutation follow the same rule, and the kind zoo is
  sugar over the lattice.

‚úĽ Baked for 1m 16s

‚ĚĮ but the other day we decided                                                  
                                                                                
  system Query (pos, var) eff {}                                                
                                                                                
  for                                                                           
                                                                                
  system named-or-inline-query (mutables) eff-flag                              

‚óŹ Right ‚ÄĒ that factors it more cleanly than my Bevy-style &mut-in-the-query. The
  decided shape:

  system  <named-or-inline-query>  (<mutables>)  <eff-flag>  { ... }
  //       read/access set          write subset   effect cap   body

  system Query (pos, var) eff {} = query Query is the access set, (pos, var) is
  the &mut subset, eff grants effect permission, body checked against all three.

  Why the mutables are a separate list, not &mut inside the query

  This is the part I got wrong, and the factoring has a real reason. Bevy puts
  &mut in the query because Bevy queries are inline, per-system. arche queries
  are named and shared ‚ÄĒ Movers :: query { pos, vel } gets reused by many
  systems, and mutability is per-use: one system writes vel, another only reads
  it. So mutability can't live in the shared query; it has to hoist out to the
  system's use-site. Hence the separate (mutables) list. The query is the
  sharable column set; (mutables) is this system's write permission over it.
  That's strictly better than my version for exactly the named-query reason.

  The signature is the whole permission set ‚ÄĒ read without the body

  So all three permission classes sit in the signature, none derived from the
  body:
  - read set ‚Üí the query (named or inline)
  - write set ‚Üí (mutables)
  - effect cap ‚Üí eff flag

  Placer and scheduler read this signature alone ‚Üí separately compilable, no
  body inference, no hidden color. The body is checked against it (write outside
  (mutables) ‚Üí error; eff without running one ‚Üí dead flag). Exactly the Rust
  borrow-check discipline, just with the mut-set hoisted and effects as a flag
  instead of a type.

  The eff flag is the socket for the branch thread

  The thing we circled earlier lands here for free: eff is the first capability
  flag, and branch (the GPU-divergence axis) is just the next flag in that same
  slot. So the kind presets fall straight out of the flag combination:

  - map (GPU-legal) = system <query> (<mutables>) with no eff, no branch
  - the unnamed "divergent kernel" corner = branch but no eff (pure, branchy,
  GPU-with-masking)
  - each/system = eff + branch

  The decided syntax already has the socket ‚ÄĒ you don't need new machinery to
  name the divergent-kernel corner, just another flag alongside eff. So "one
  system + a permission signature" and "graded branch power" are the same
  design: the flags are the lattice, and which flags you raise is the explicit
  ask that decides placement.

  Is branch meant to be an explicit flag in that slot too, or did we leave
  control-flow folded into eff for now?

‚úĽ Crunched for 59s
‚ĚĮ okay so now back to my earlier question, does it make sense to have a branch  
  flag? or does branchless not offer anything interesting                       

‚óŹ Short answer: yes, but only as a hard structural gate ‚ÄĒ and what branchless 
  buys is the one thing arche's static placement actually can't do without.

  What branchless actually offers (in arche-relevance order)

  1. (textbook) zero divergence. A data-dependent branch where SIMT lanes
  disagree executes both sides masked ‚ÄĒ up to N√ó waste. Branchless = guaranteed
  full lane width on GPU and AVX. Known, real, but not the interesting part.
  2. (the one that matters here) bounded, statically-computable cost. This is
  the payoff. A branchy kernel's cost depends on the data ‚ÄĒ worst with a
  data-dependent loop, where the trip count is unknown at compile time, so the
  cost isn't just variable, it's potentially unbounded. A static placer faced
  with that has two options: analyze the body (breaks separate compilation) or
  assume the worst (never confidently picks GPU). Branchless removes the
  control-flow contribution to cost entirely ‚Üí the per-element cost is
  computable from the signature.

  That second point is the whole game, because of ¬ß5: arche threw away the 
  runtime scheduler. StarPU doesn't need branchless ‚ÄĒ it measures cost at
  runtime and adapts. arche decided placement is static, so it depends on a cost
  it can compute at compile time, and branchless is precisely the regime where
  it can. So branchless isn't "a nice optimization" here; it's the property that
  makes the no-runtime-scheduler placement decision decidable instead of merely
  bounded. That's the genuinely interesting thing, and it's arche-specific.

  Why it must be a flag, and why a hard one

  By the principle we just settled: the placer reads the signature, not the
  body. Branchlessness is placement-relevant, so it has to be in the signature ‚ÄĒ
  derive it from the body and you break separate compilation and re-hide the
  color, exactly as deriving mut would. Consistency check passes: branch is a
  third capability alongside read-set / mutables / eff.

  But there's a category trap to avoid. eff is a legality gate ‚ÄĒ effects
  literally cannot go to the GPU. Branch is a performance gate ‚ÄĒ a branchy pure
  kernel is perfectly legal on the GPU, just slower. If you encode branch as a
  soft "this might be slow" hint, it's exactly the noise your
  hard-errors-over-lints ethos rejects. The only version that earns its place is
  the hard one: a system without the branch flag is structurally forbidden from
  data-dependent control flow (your existing E0046 on map, generalized to a
  flag). Then no-flag isn't advice ‚ÄĒ it's a checkable prohibition that licenses 
  the placer to rely on bounded cost + zero divergence. The prohibition earns
  the guarantee, the same way map earns GPU-eligibility today by forbidding
  branches.

  Does branchless offer "nothing"? Only if you don't need exact cost

  Here's the honest crux, and it's your call:

  - If your static placer needs real cost ("compute CPU-vs-GPU profitability
  statically"), the branch flag is load-bearing ‚ÄĒ it's the bit that separates
  the costable class from the uncostable one.
  - If your placer will be conservative anyway (rough heuristic from R/W + eff,
  never an exact cost model), then branch buys you nothing at placement time ‚ÄĒ
  only backend vectorization quality, which the backend can attempt
  opportunistically without a flag. In that world it is ceremony.

  And it can't be folded into eff: a pure, no-eff system can still branch on
  column data (if pos.x > 0 { vel.x = -vel.x }). eff=no, branch=yes is a real,
  common kernel ‚ÄĒ the empty corner. So with only the eff flag you literally
  cannot distinguish the GPU-ideal kernel from the GPU-divergent one. Branch
  carries independent information or it's invisible.

  Recommendation

  Include it, as the hard gate ‚ÄĒ for one reason that dominates: it's the only
  flag that directly serves the ¬ß5 unsolved problem. Static placement needs
  bounded static cost; the branch prohibition is what delivers it; nothing else
  in the signature does. It costs one bit and one structural check (you already
  have the check ‚ÄĒ it's E0046), and the alternative is that your hottest, most
  placeable kernels (game.step and every physics/transform/blend loop) are
  indistinguishable from divergent ones at the signature the placer reads.

  The real question back to you is the crux above: how exact does the static 
  cost model have to be? If "exact enough to flip a placement decision," branch
  is essential. If "rough heuristic," it's optional polish.

  because in general everything should ideally be maps over data and not

‚óŹ This is the real question, and the user wants examples ‚ÄĒ let me pull the
  actual arche-rpg and web-server source so I'm analyzing real control flow, not
  the doc's sketches. I'll explore both in parallel and handle the literature
  alongside.

‚óŹ 2 background agents launched (‚Üď to manage)
   ‚Ēú Map control flow in arche-rpg
   ‚ĒĒ Map control flow in web server example

‚óŹ Both Explore agents are running against the real source. While they inventory
  the actual control flow, here's the conceptual frame and the literature, so
  the code findings drop into a ready structure.

  The core move you're describing has a precise name

  Replacing data-dependent control flow with "maps over data +
  producer/consumer" is exactly synchronous dataflow: reify control state into a
  data column, and replace "do A, then based on the result do B" with a fixed 
  network of maps re-evaluated each tick, where the "then" becomes "next tick,
  the state column says where we are." Control flow doesn't get executed; it
  gets stored.

  Two reifications do almost all the work:

  1. State-machine-as-data. A per-connection read header ‚Üí read body ‚Üí respond
  monadic sequence becomes a state column (READING_HEADER | READING_BODY | DONE)
  plus one uniform map/each that advances every connection one step per tick
  based on its state. The sequencing is gone; the "where am I" is data. This is
  how Lustre/Esterel eliminate control flow ‚ÄĒ time and state are data, the
  program is a fixed equation network.
  2. Branch-as-routing (partition/compaction). if cond { do_x } over a
  collection becomes "compact the cond-true rows into a run, map over the run" ‚ÄĒ
  the branch becomes which pool/column you land in, i.e. producer/consumer. The
  divergence is replaced by a partition.

  Papers, on exactly this topic

  - Lee & Messerschmitt, Synchronous Data Flow, Proc. IEEE 1987 ‚ÄĒ the
  foundational one. Producer/consumer with statically-known rates ‚Üí statically
  schedulable, bounded buffers, no data-dependent firing. This is your
  producer/consumer pattern with the data-dependence removed, and it's the model
  that makes it statically placeable (ties straight to the
  branch-flag/static-cost thread).
  - Halbwachs, Caspi, Raymond, Pilaud, The Synchronous Dataflow Programming 
  Language LUSTRE, Proc. IEEE 1991 ‚ÄĒ "everything is a stream equation, no
  control flow, statically scheduled." Esterel/SCADE are the same family. The
  deepest match for "everything should be maps over data."
  - Thies, Karczmarek, Amarasinghe, StreamIt, CC 2002 ‚ÄĒ filters (maps) +
  split/join, statically scheduled; control flow eliminated into stream
  structure.
  - Blelloch, Vector Models for Data-Parallel Computing (1990) and Prefix Sums 
  and Their Applications ‚ÄĒ scan/segmented-scan as the primitive that replaces
  control flow; the canonical "how to do the thing you'd branch for,
  branchlessly."
  - Blelloch & Sabot, Compiling Collection-Oriented Languages‚Ä¶ (NESL flattening)
  ‚ÄĒ flattening turns irregular/nested (control-flow-shaped) parallelism into
  flat segmented data-parallel ops. Literally "remove the control flow data
  irregularity would induce."
  - Stream compaction: Billeter, Olsson, Assarsson, Efficient Stream Compaction 
  on Wide SIMD (HPG¬†2009) ‚ÄĒ the GPU realization of branch-as-partition.
  - Kahn Process Networks (Kahn¬†1974) ‚ÄĒ the formal producer/consumer model, but
  note the contrast: KPN allows blocking, data-dependent reads (deterministic
  but unbounded), whereas SDF restricts to static rates. arche wants the SDF
  end, not the KPN end ‚ÄĒ that distinction is exactly your "remove data-dependent
  control flow" line.
  - Valiant, BSP (1990) ‚ÄĒ supersteps + barriers, the
  schedule-of-systems-with-barriers structure arche already has.

  The through-line: control flow you can't remove is exactly the data-dependence
  SDF forbids and KPN permits. Whether arche-rpg and the server fall on the SDF
  side is the empirical question the agents are answering now ‚ÄĒ I'll map each
  construct they find onto {already a map / state-machine-able / genuinely
  KPN-monadic}.

  I'll synthesize the verdict per program once both agents report back.

  ‚ĒĒ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēė

  The important fact: there is no type (a) (result-dependent-on-an-effect / 
  monadic) control flow anywhere in arche-rpg. The clamps are predication
  (min/max). The per-pixel loops are bounded geometric iteration ‚ÄĒ which is the
  canonical data-parallel case: rasterizing a circle is a map over the
  bounding-box pixels with a predicated if inside { write }, exactly how a GPU
  does it.

  The one real cost, and it's pure arche-deal: a map over a fixed max bounding
  box ([2¬∑R_max]¬≤) predicated, instead of a tight data-dependent loop ‚ÄĒ you
  process the ceiling extent always. That's the same "no heap = everything
  max-reserved, pay the ceiling up front" trade you make everywhere else
  (flat-effect ¬ß8). So arche-rpg is fully expressible with no data-dependent 
  control flow at the system level, at the price of predicated max-extent maps.

  The genuinely interesting question ‚ÄĒ whether the server can shed its type (a)
  monadic sequencing (framed reads, accept loop, per-connection state) ‚ÄĒ is what
  the second agent is resolving now. I'll give the combined verdict + where
  this lands against the SDF/KPN line once it reports.

  The genuinely-monadic surface is one pattern, and it dissolves

  ‚Ēā parse_request_line,   ‚Ēā pure branches on  ‚Ēā no ‚ÄĒ pure logic, and the   ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā mime_by_ext, reason   ‚Ēā buffer/status     ‚Ēā last two are already funcs ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā                       ‚Ēā                   ‚Ēā  (you excepted funcs)      ‚Ēā

  ‚Ēā router.resolve        ‚Ēā data-dependent    ‚Ēā no ‚ÄĒ pure matching, zero   ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā                       ‚Ēā loop + breaks     ‚Ēā effects (type c, in-tick)  ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā socket_loopback       ‚Ēā                   ‚Ēā no ‚ÄĒ a value, not control  ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā recv(‚Ä¶)(buf,r:) then  ‚Ēā data-dependent    ‚Ēā flow (the flat-effect      ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā buf[0:r]              ‚Ēā slice bound       ‚Ēā "slice-slot" deferred      ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā                       ‚Ēā                   ‚Ēā case)                      ‚Ēā
  ‚ĒĒ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēė

  The only genuinely-monadic-over-effects pattern is the one the flat-effect doc
  sketches but the real code doesn't even contain yet: the framed read (read 
  header ‚Üí read that-many bytes) and the accept loop. So the whole question
  reduces to those two ‚ÄĒ and both reframe cleanly.

  The reframe: the monadic "then" becomes a tick boundary + a state column

  The framed read as an epoll-style per-connection state machine as data ‚ÄĒ one
  uniform each advances every connection one non-blocking step per tick:

  arche
  // design sketch
  Conn :: arche { sock, state, want, have, buf }   // state: NEED_HDR |
  NEED_BODY | DONE
  step :: each (query { sock, state, want, have, buf }) (state, want, have, buf)
  {
    recv(sock, want - have)(chunk, n:);            // ONE non-blocking effect;
  result is DATA
    have += n;
    if state == NEED_HDR && have >= HDR { want = HDR + len_of(buf); state =
  NEED_BODY; }
    if state == NEED_BODY && have >= want { state = DONE; }   // pure branches
  on the state column
  }
  #run forever(seq({ accept_step, step, dispatch_done }))     // monadic bind ‚Č°
  next tick

  The read X then read Y(result-of-X) sequencing is gone ‚ÄĒ it's now the
  state/want/have columns, advanced uniformly. You never sequence two effects
  within a system on the first's result; you do one effect, store its result as
  data, and the schedule re-fires the uniform step. Monadic bind ‚áí a tick 
  boundary. The accept loop is the same: an accept_step that does one
  non-blocking accept per tick and inserts a Conn row (producer) drained by step
  (consumer).

  Verdict

  Yes ‚ÄĒ both arche-rpg and the server are fully expressible with no 
  result-dependent-on-effect control flow at the system level, with zero genuine
  monadic-bind-in-a-system required:

  - arche-rpg: no effect-control-flow at all; all branches are pure geometry ‚Üí
  predicated max-extent maps.
  - server: the one monadic pattern (framed read / accept) ‚Üí epoll-style
  state-machine-as-data + producer/consumer pools.

  What does NOT vanish, and shouldn't:
  1. Pure data-dependent loops in parsing/matching/rasterizing (router.resolve,
  parse_request_line, the pixel loops). These are bounded, effect-free in-tick
  iteration. They predicate/flatten or live in funcs ‚ÄĒ but irregular parsing has
  genuine data-dependent structure; you don't delete it, you flatten it.
  2. Effect results as data values (buf[0:r], recv counts). Not control flow ‚ÄĒ
  and you want these.

  So the defensible rule isn't "no data-dependent control flow at all" (too
  strong ‚ÄĒ kills parsing/rasterizing) but exactly the line the flat-effect model
  already draws ‚ÄĒ no value-level bind; no result-dependent sequencing of two 
  effects inside a system ‚ÄĒ and the evidence says you can push even the
  documented handle_msg holdout all the way into ECS decomposition, so the
  inline-monadic-system never has to exist. The cost is the
  event-loop/state-machine discipline instead of blocking sequential reads ‚ÄĒ
  which arche's no-runtime-scheduler, data-oriented nature wants anyway.

  Papers ‚ÄĒ the bullseye ones for this (servers + parsing), beyond the dataflow 
  set

  - Adya, Howell, Theimer, Bolosky, Douceur ‚ÄĒ Cooperative Task Management 
  without Manual Stack Management, USENIX ATC¬†2002. "Stack ripping" ‚ÄĒ the exact
  transformation of blocking sequential effect code into an event-driven state 
  machine. This is the formal name for your "monadic then ‚Üí state column + tick"
  move. The money citation.
  - Welsh, Culler, Brewer ‚ÄĒ SEDA, SOSP¬†2001. A server as a pipeline of stages
  connected by queues = producer/consumer pools + systems. The architectural
  match for "server as data-parallel stages."
  - *von Behren, Condit, Brewer ‚ÄĒ Why Events Are A Bad Idea (for 
  High-Concurrency Servers), HotOS¬†2003.* The honest counterweight ‚ÄĒ argues the
  threads/events duality, i.e. what you lose (readability) when you rip the
  stack into a state machine. Worth reading for the cost side.
  - Mytkowicz, Musuvathi, Schulte ‚ÄĒ Data-Parallel Finite-State Machines, ASPLOS 
  2014, and Langdale & Lemire ‚ÄĒ Parsing Gigabytes of JSON per Second (simdjson),
  VLDB J.¬†2019. Directly on whether the parsing loops (parse_request_line,
  router) can be de-branched/data-parallelized ‚ÄĒ the answer to "can the pure
  data-dependent loops also go branchless."

  The clean way to see it: removing data-dependent effect-control-flow = moving
  from the KPN end (blocking, data-dependent consumption) to the SDF end
  (static-rate, state-machine, event-loop). Stack-ripping (Adya) is the
  mechanical transformation; SEDA is the architecture; arche's
  schedule-of-systems-over-pools is already sitting at that end.

  Want me to write this up ‚ÄĒ the framed-read-as-state-machine pattern and the
  "no monadic bind in a system, push to ECS" rule ‚ÄĒ as a section in the WIP doc,
  or keep going on the analysis (e.g., whether the pure parsing loops should
  also be forced branchless)?

‚úĽ Baked for 5m 51s

‚ĚĮ so in theory we could remove control flow from everything. which largely      
  means branchless for everything except funcs and policies?                    

‚óŹ Yes ‚ÄĒ with one precision that makes it exact: in the data-parallel/scheduled
  world, "branchless" doesn't mean control flow is gone, it means control flow 
  is reified into data. Three reifications cover everything we found:

  - predication ‚ÄĒ if c {x} ‚Üí select(c, x, ‚Ä¶) (the rasterizer clamps, distance
  test)
  - tables ‚ÄĒ a state machine or dispatch is next = table[state][input], a
  lookup, not a branch (the framed-read FSM, HTTP method dispatch)
  - partitioning / routing ‚ÄĒ if c { do_x } over rows ‚Üí "rows where c land in
  pool X" = producer/consumer (parse routing to Bad/Routed)

  Once branches are data, the leaves (map/each/system over pools) are
  straight-line. What's left as genuine control flow lives in exactly your two
  places.

  This resolves the branch-flag question ‚ÄĒ by removing the flag

  If leaves are structurally branchless (E0046 generalized from map to every
  leaf), you don't need the branch capability flag we debated. The leaf/func 
  boundary becomes the branch gate ‚ÄĒ hard and structural, not a per-kernel flag.
  The "pure divergent kernel" corner we found empty stays empty by design, and
  the payoff is unconditional: every leaf is cost-exact and GPU-legal by
  construction, no signature bit required. That's the strongest version of the
  earlier thread, and it's cleaner than a flag.

  Two honest caveats

  1. Irreducible pure control flow ‚Üí funcs, and it's CPU-bound.
  Recursive-descent parsing, tree/graph walks, the router.resolve matching loop
  ‚ÄĒ these resist cheap tabling/predication. They must live in funcs (where
  recursion is free by design ‚ÄĒ flat-effect ¬ß1). That's consistent, but it means
  such logic isn't GPU-placeable; it runs CPU-side. Funcs are the sanctioned
  home for the control flow you genuinely can't reify.

  2. A branchy func taints its caller's GPU placement. This is the subtle one. A
  branchless each that calls a branchy func, when inlined for the GPU, drags
  the func's branches back into the kernel ‚Üí divergence reappears. So "funcs may
  branch freely" is true for CPU but not free for GPU: a branchy func makes any
  leaf calling it CPU-only. And to keep separate compilation, that has to be
  readable from the func's signature ‚ÄĒ i.e. branch-freedom becomes a color on
  funcs (straight-line vs branchy), propagated like an effect color, so a leaf's
  GPU-eligibility is decidable from signatures alone without whole-program
  inlining analysis. So the branch flag you cut from leaves reappears, smaller,
  as a branchless/straight-line color on funcs ‚ÄĒ which is where it belongs,
  since funcs are the only thing left that branches.

  On "policies"

  I'm reading "policy" as the hoisted decision points ‚ÄĒ pool overflow (?abort),
  the placement cost-model, schedule loop-end gating (when(should_quit, halt)),
  mode selection ‚ÄĒ control that's scalar/singular, not per-element, sitting
  outside the data-parallel hot path. That's the classic mechanism-vs-policy
  split: branchless mechanism in the leaves, branchy decisions concentrated in
  named policies. (If by "policy" you mean something narrower ‚ÄĒ a specific arche
  construct ‚ÄĒ tell me and I'll re-fit.)

  So the taxonomy holds, sharpened: leaves are branchless (control flow reified 
  as predication/tables/routing); irreducible control flow lives in funcs as a 
  propagated straight-line/branchy color that gates GPU placement; orchestration
  decisions live in policies. The data-parallel world never branches; it
  indexes data that encodes the branch.

  The one thing I'd want to nail down before this is a clean rule: is the
  func-side branchless color something you want to track and enforce (so
  GPU-placement is signature-decidable), or are you content to let branchy funcs
  simply pin their callers to CPU and not chase GPU through funcs at all?
  That's the fork that decides how much machinery this costs.

‚úĽ Cooked for 1m 49s

‚ĚĮ what? runtime dispatch?                                                       

‚óŹ Good catch ‚ÄĒ "dispatch" was a sloppy word. No, nothing here is runtime 
  dispatch. Let me separate the two things I blurred:

  - Data-table lookup ‚ÄĒ next_state[i] = T[state[i], input[i]]. This yields a
  value (the next state, a route tag, a handler id). It's an array load, exactly
  like reading pos[i]. The program's control-flow graph is fixed; only a datum
  is looked up. Not dispatch.
  - Function-pointer / vtable jump ‚ÄĒ call vtable[tag](...). That's runtime
  dispatch, and arche forbids it (static dispatch, monomorphized, no runtime
  fn-pointers ‚ÄĒ flat-effect ¬ß6.3). I am not proposing this.

  The FSM table is the first kind. It produces the next state as data and stores
  it in a column. No indirect call.

  How behavior selection happens without a jump

  When different states need different work, you don't jump to different code
  per row. Two static options:

  1. Predicate and select ‚ÄĒ run the (few, cheap) state-bodies, select by the
  state value. Branchless, but does all bodies' work per row.
  2. Partition by state ‚ÄĒ bin rows so all NEED_HDR rows are contiguous, all
  NEED_BODY contiguous, then a statically-scheduled system per bucket drains
  each. The "dispatch" becomes which pool a row sits in + which 
  compile-time-named system processes that pool. The schedule names every system
  at compile time ‚ÄĒ same as the route example, where parse routes rows to
  Bad/Routed pools and static systems drain them.

  Either way the schedule and the call targets are fixed at compile time. The
  state, the route, the handler-id are all data; the code that runs is
  statically known. That's precisely arche's existing "static and local,
  compile-time vtable, no runtime search" ‚ÄĒ the FSM table lives inside that
  model, it doesn't break it.

  The honest cost

  Option 2 ‚ÄĒ avoiding both runtime dispatch and the predication-waste of option
  1 ‚ÄĒ needs a binning/sort pass to group rows by state before the per-bucket
  kernels run. That's a scan/sort (Blelloch), the data-parallel FSM technique
  (Mytkowicz, Data-Parallel Finite-State Machines, ASPLOS 2014): you never
  dispatch per element; you sort by state and map homogeneous runs with
  statically-known kernels. So the price of "no runtime dispatch" is a sort, not
  an indirect call.

  So: table = data load (fine), behavior = predication or static partition
  (fine), and the only new cost is a binning pass. Did you read "table" as a
  jump-table of code pointers? Because that's the thing we both want to keep
  out.

‚úĽ Churned for 50s

‚ĚĮ what is the point of this  data table lookup?                                 

‚óŹ Honestly? For the programs we're looking at, not much ‚ÄĒ I oversold it. Let me
  be straight about when the table earns its keep and when it's ceremony.

  The table's only real point is uniformity at width: when you run one state
  machine across a huge population in lockstep on SIMD/GPU, next = 
  T[state][input] is an O(1) identical load for every lane regardless of how
  many states exist ‚ÄĒ zero divergence even with 50 states. A predicated
  if-ladder, by contrast, costs proportional to the number of arms. That's the
  simdjson / data-parallel-FSM regime: parsing gigabytes, millions of elements
  in lockstep. There the table is the technique.

  For the web server and arche-rpg, that's not the regime. The connection FSM
  has ~3 states over a handful (or even thousands) of connections on a CPU.
  There the table buys nothing, and the other two reifications are simply
  better:

  - the 2‚Äď3 states ‚Üí just predicate them in one small each (the compiler emits
  the selects), or
  - partition: NEED_HDR rows in one pool, NEED_BODY in another, a static system
  draining each.

  So I should retract framing the framed-read as "a table" ‚ÄĒ it's
  predication-or-partition. The table is the third tool and it's niche.

  The general rule, which is the actual answer: reifying control flow into data
  ‚ÄĒ table, predicate, or partition ‚ÄĒ is only worth it to keep a leaf branchless,
  and the only reason you want a leaf branchless is to buy GPU-placeability +
  static cost (the ¬ß5 problem). If a piece of logic isn't going wide and doesn't
  need a compile-time cost, making it branchless is pure overhead ‚ÄĒ put the 
  control flow in a func and run it on the CPU. The table isn't a goal; it's a
  price you pay for placeability on one specific kernel.

  So the point of the data-table lookup is exactly as narrow as "I have a
  stateful computation I want to run branchless across a massive lockstep
  population." If you don't have that, you don't want the table ‚ÄĒ and most of
  arche-rpg and the server don't.

  Which makes me think the real question underneath is: which kernels in these 
  programs actually want to go wide? Because that's the only set for which any
  of this reification (table or otherwise) is justified ‚ÄĒ everything else just
  uses funcs and stays on the CPU.

‚úĽ Cogitated for 50s

‚ĚĮ give a concrete example because it sounds like nonsense                       

‚óŹ Fair ‚ÄĒ here's the concrete version, with numbers, and I'll show exactly where
  it's real and where it's pointless.

  The problem: scan a byte stream, mark which chars are inside a "‚Ä¶" string

  Two states: OUT=0, IN=1. The branchy, per-byte version:

  arche
  // sequential: state[i] depends on state[i-1] ‚ÄĒ CANNOT parallelize
  if state == OUT { if c == '"' { state = IN } }
  else            { if c == '"' { state = OUT } }

  This is inherently sequential ‚ÄĒ every byte needs the previous byte's state. A
  branch is not a value, so you can't hand it to a parallel scan. That's the
  wall.

  The table turns "what this byte does to the state" into a value

  Represent each byte's effect as a 2-vector over states [next_if_OUT, 
  next_if_IN]:

  - a non-quote byte ‚Üí [0,1] (identity: OUT‚ÜíOUT, IN‚ÜíIN)
  - a " byte ‚Üí [1,0] (swap: OUT‚ÜíIN, IN‚ÜíOUT)

  Now composition of two byte-effects f then g is just a gather: (g‚ąėf)[s] = 
  g[f[s]]. No branch ‚ÄĒ an indexed load. And composition is associative, so a
  parallel prefix-scan computes, for every position, the composed effect from
  the start, branchlessly.

  Worked, on a"b"c

  byte-effects: a=[0,1]  "=[1,0]  b=[0,1]  "=[1,0]  c=[0,1]

  scan-compose left‚Üíright, then apply to start OUT=0 (final[i] = prefix[i][0]):

  ‚ĒĆ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēź
  ‚Ēā  i  ‚Ēā byte ‚Ēā prefix vector ‚Ēā final[i] = prefix[0] ‚Ēā    meaning     ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā 0   ‚Ēā a    ‚Ēā [0,1]         ‚Ēā 0 = OUT              ‚Ēā a is outside ‚úď ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā 1   ‚Ēā "    ‚Ēā [1,0]         ‚Ēā 1 = IN               ‚Ēā opened ‚úď       ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā 2   ‚Ēā b    ‚Ēā [1,0]         ‚Ēā 1 = IN               ‚Ēā b is inside ‚úď  ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā 3   ‚Ēā "    ‚Ēā [0,1]         ‚Ēā 0 = OUT              ‚Ēā closed ‚úď       ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā 4   ‚Ēā c    ‚Ēā [0,1]         ‚Ēā 0 = OUT              ‚Ēā c is outside ‚úď ‚Ēā
  ‚ĒĒ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēė

  Matches the sequential run exactly ‚ÄĒ but the scan is O(log n) depth and every
  operation is a gather, zero branches. The table is the only reason this works:
  you composed transition functions as data. You cannot put an if inside a
  scan's combiner; you can put a table-gather. That's the entire point, and it's
  not nonsense ‚ÄĒ it's how simdjson finds string regions in JSON at
  gigabytes/sec (prefix-scan over character-class transitions).

  Where it's pointless ‚ÄĒ and most of your code is here

  For the web server's 3-state connection FSM over a few thousand connections:
  there's no long sequential stream to scan, each connection is independent, and
  the per-tick step is cheap. The scan-table machinery buys nothing. You just
  partition (rows in NEED_HDR pool vs NEED_BODY pool, a static system draining
  each) or predicate the 3 cases. Same for the rasterizer ‚ÄĒ predicated clamps,
  no table.

  So the rule, concretely: the data-table-as-value is worth it iff you have a 
  long, inherently-sequential dependency you need to run in parallel
  (byte/stream parsing at scale). That's the one shape where a branch literally
  can't be parallelized and a table can. Everywhere else ‚ÄĒ independent rows,
  cheap per-element work ‚ÄĒ it's overhead, and you use predication, partitioning,
  or a plain func.

  So: the table isn't a general "avoid branches" tool. It's specifically the
  trick for parallelizing a sequential state machine, and the web server /
  arche-rpg almost entirely don't have that shape.

‚úĽ Cogitated for 1m 2s

‚ĚĮ stop yapping, just show examples of code of the control flow version and the  
  non control flow version and why it needs the table thing                     

‚óŹ Control-flow version ‚ÄĒ sequential, branchy, can't parallelize (each byte needs
  the previous state):

  arche
  state := OUT;
  for i in 0..n {
    if state == OUT {
      if buf[i] == '"' { state = IN }
    } else {
      if buf[i] == '"' { state = OUT }
    }
    mark[i] = state;          // mark[i] needs mark[i-1] ‚Üí strictly serial
  }

  Table version, still serial ‚ÄĒ same logic, branch replaced by a lookup:

  arche
  // T[state*2 + class] : class 0 = other, 1 = quote
  T := [ /*OUT*/ OUT, IN,   /*IN*/ IN, OUT ];

  state := OUT;
  for i in 0..n {
    class := select(buf[i] == '"', 1, 0);   // branchless compare
    state  = T[state*2 + class];             // one load, no branch
    mark[i] = state;                          // STILL serial: state threads
  through
  }

  The table alone doesn't buy parallelism ‚ÄĒ it's still state ‚Üź f(state). Here's
  why you actually need it:

  No-control-flow parallel version ‚ÄĒ make each byte's effect a value (a 2-vector
  [next_if_OUT, next_if_IN]), then scan-compose:

  arche
  // step 1: map each byte to its transition vector ‚ÄĒ fully parallel, branchless
  eff :: map (query { byte, vec }) {
    q := select(byte == '"', 1, 0);
    vec = select(q, [IN, OUT], [OUT, IN]);   // quote=swap, other=identity
  }

  // step 2: associative compose of two transition vectors = a gather (no
  branch)
  compose :: func(f: [2]int, g: [2]int) -> [2]int {
    return [ g[f[0]], g[f[1]] ];             // (g‚ąėf)[s] = g[f[s]]
  }

  // step 3: parallel prefix-scan with `compose`, then apply to start state
  scan :: scan (query { vec }) using compose;       // O(log n) depth, parallel
  emit :: map (query { vec, mark }) { mark = vec[OUT]; }   // apply to start =
  OUT

  Why the table is required: a parallel scan needs an associative combine over 
  values. compose(f, g) works because a transition is a vector you can gather
  through. You cannot put if state == OUT {‚Ä¶} inside compose ‚ÄĒ a branch isn't a
  value, has no ‚ąė. The table is what turns "the control flow this byte triggers"
  into data the scan can fold. That's the whole reason it exists.

  Without that, the FSM is the serial loop at top. With it, it's three
  branchless data-parallel kernels.

‚úĽ Baked for 17s
‚ĚĮ so it's a design implementation and not a language feature                    

‚óŹ Correct. The table+scan is a design pattern built from primitives arche 
  already has ‚ÄĒ arrays (the table), select, a func (compose), and scan over a
  column. No new syntax, no new kind, nothing the compiler has to know about.
  It's just how you'd choose to write a wide FSM.

  The only actual language questions in this whole thread are two:

  1. Are leaves structurally branchless? (generalize map's E0046 to all
  data-parallel leaves ‚ÄĒ control flow only in funcs/policies)
  2. A branchless/straight-line color on funcs, so a leaf's GPU-placeability is
  decidable from signatures alone (separate compilation).

  Everything else ‚ÄĒ tables, predication, partitioning, scan-FSM ‚ÄĒ is design on
  top of existing primitives, not a feature to build.

‚úĽ Cogitated for 18s

‚ĚĮ explain the compose keyword in that example                                   

‚óŹ compose isn't a keyword ‚ÄĒ it's the ordinary func I defined two lines above and
  then passed to scan as its combine operator (the way + is the operator for a
  sum-scan). The using compose just names which func the scan folds with.

  What it computes: each transition is a vector [next_if_OUT, next_if_IN].
  compose(f, g) = the single vector equivalent to "apply f, then g":

  arche
  compose :: func(f: [2]int, g: [2]int) -> [2]int {
    return [ g[f[0]], g[f[1]] ];   // in state s: f sends s‚Üíf[s], then g sends
  that‚Üíg[f[s]]
  }

  g[f[s]] is two array indexes (gathers), no branch.

  Concrete: f = [1,0] (quote, swaps) then g = [1,0] (quote again):
  compose(f,g) = [ g[f[0]], g[f[1]] ] = [ g[1], g[0] ] = [0,1] = identity. Two
  quotes cancel. ‚úď

  Why scan can use it: function composition is associative ‚ÄĒ (h‚ąėg)‚ąėf = h‚ąė(g‚ąėf) ‚ÄĒ
  which is the one requirement for a parallel prefix-scan. So scanning with
  compose gives, at every position, the total transition from the start;
  vec[OUT] then applies it to the initial state. Same role + plays in a
  prefix-sum, just over transition vectors instead of numbers.

  So: regular func, used as the scan's associative operator. Nothing special in
  the language.
